class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Transition+Metal+Dichalcogenides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_transition%20metal%20dichalcogenides">transition metal dichalcogenides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fese2+Microspheres&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_FeSe2%20microspheres">FeSe<sub>2</sub> microspheres</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sulfur-doped+Reduced+Graphene+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sulfur-doped%20reduced%20graphene%20oxide">sulfur-doped reduced graphene oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Synergistic+Effect&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_synergistic%20effect">synergistic effect</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Owing to their superior electrochemical property, rechargeable lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been employed as a technology of choice for electric vehicles and portable electronics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Nevertheless, large scale applications of LIBs have been hindered by their expensive price and the uneven geological distribution of lithium resources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 2 and 3">(2, 3)</a> In sharp contrast to LIBs, sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted widespread interest because of abundant terrestrial reserves of sodium mineral salts and their physical/chemical properties analogous with LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4-8)</a> However, the structural variability of anodes is limited by the higher ionization potential and larger ionic diameter of Na ions, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9-11)</a> which make Na<sup>+</sup> difficult to insert into the graphitic layers structure of carbonaceous materials generally employed as anodes for LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 12 to 15">(12-15)</a> Therefore, exploring advanced anode materials with low cost and high reversible capacity for SIBs to achieve superior electrochemical performance is urgently desirable but remains a great challenge.</div><div class="NLM_p">Currently, considerable attention has been paid to fundamental research of transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) as anodes for SIBs due to their high theoretical capacities and widespread availability, such as MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> MoSe<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> TiS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Meanwhile, FeS<sub>2</sub> also has attracted much attention for its abundant resources, cheap price, chemical stability, nontoxicity, high theoretical capacity, and being environmentally benign. Nowadays, various nanostructured FeS<sub>2</sub> have been applied as anode materials for SIBs to investigate their electrochemical performances and reaction mechanism. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 20 to 26">(20-26)</a> However, to the best of our knowledge, FeSe<sub>2</sub> has been rarely reported as anode material for SIBs, which have better electric conductivity than FeS<sub>2</sub> and similar physicochemical properties with FeS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27, 28)</a> Chen et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> first demonstrated that usage of FeSe<sub>2</sub> as anode materials for SIBs in ether-based electrolyte can display an excellent rate performance and a large reversible capacity of 447 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, verifying that FeSe<sub>2</sub> is a promising candidate for SIBs. Nonetheless, similar to the other TMDs, the practical application of FeSe<sub>2</sub> for SIBs is still restricted by the low electric conductivity and large volume expansion/contraction in the discharge/charge process owing to the conversion reaction mechanism, which leads to dramatic capacity fading and low rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> To overcome these disadvantages, an effective method is to decorate TMDs materials with conductive graphene matrixes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 32 to 36">(32-36)</a> The presence of graphene can alleviate the volume change, enhance the electric conductivity, and shorten the path length of Na ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37-39)</a> To further effectively modulate the band structure and improve the electrical properties of graphene, continuous efforts have been carried out to fabricate heteroatom-doped graphene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 40 to 43">(40-43)</a> among which sulfur doping is considered as one of the most effective methods. Chen et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> reported that graphene nanosheets covalently bonded with sulfur atoms as anode for SIBs can keep a long cyclic stability and an outstanding rate performance with the reversible capacity of 291, 127, and 83 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> under the current of 0.05, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Taking these factors into consideration, we combine the high theoretical capacity materials FeSe<sub>2</sub> with sulfur-doped reduced graphene oxide (rGO) sheets as an anode material of SIBs for the first time to achieve an excellent cycle performance and a remarkable rate capability simultaneously.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we design a facile strategy to embed nanooctahedra particles assembled FeSe<sub>2</sub> microspheres into sulfur-doped rGO sheets through a one-step hydrothermal reduction route, in which we realize the reduction of graphene oxide, the doping of sulfur atoms, and the preparation of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/sulfur-doped rGO (FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG) composites at the same time. Furthermore, for the first time, we employ thioacetamide as the sulfur source and reductant for the preparation of sulfur-doped rGO sheets. It is validated that the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites serving as anodes for SIBs can display superior electrochemical performances with a reversible capacity of 447.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> and an excellent rate capability with 383.3 and 277.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 2.0 and 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which could be attributed to the introduction of sulfur atoms into the rGO matrix and the synergistic effect between microsphere-like FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles and sulfur-doped rGO sheets.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A schematic is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporing Information (SI) Scheme S1</a> to describe the synthetic procedure of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites. Through the modified Hummers method, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> graphene oxide solution (GO) was obtained from flake graphite powders. To prepare the sulfur-doped GO suspensions, 0.3 g of thioacetamide acting as sulfur source and reductant at the same time was added to 40 mL of GO solution with the concentration of 7.5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup> under magnetic stirring for 12 h. In the process of synthesis FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites, 4 mmol FeCl<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O, 8 mmol SeO<sub>2</sub> powder, and 8 g of citric acid were successively added to 60 mL of distilled water. The mixture was stirred for 1 h before it was mixed with the aforementioned sulfur-doped GO suspensions. After sonication for another 1 h, 32 mL of hydrazine hydrate (80 wt %) was added dropwise to the mixture under vigorous stirring condition and then the suspensions were transferred into a Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave (200 mL) to react under the temperature of 185 °C for 12 h. Finally, the black precipitates were collected by vacuum filtration, and washed three times with deionized water and ethanol alternately. After drying for 12 h at 80 °C, the final products were obtained. As a comparison, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/rGO (FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G) samples were produced the same way but without the addition of thioacetamide and the bare FeSe<sub>2</sub> products were prepared by the similar method only replacing sulfur-doped GO suspensions with distilled water. Meanwhile, the raw rGO (G) and sulfur-doped rGO (SG) samples were synthesized by hydrothermal reduction process with hydrazine hydrate as the reductant and without adding any ferrous or selenium source.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Materials Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphologies, grain sizes, and elemental analyses of the products were measured by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, Nova NanoSEM 230) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, TecnaiG2 20ST). Energy Dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) was conducted to identify the elements existing on the surface of the samples, while Raman spectroscopy measurement was carried out on a Jobin–Yvon LabRAM HR-800 spectrometer ranging from 1000 to 3000 cm<sup>–1</sup> to identify the reduction degree of graphene oxide. The crystal structures of the materials were determined by X-ray powder diffraction (XRD, Rigaku3014), and the accurate content of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G composites were calculated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, SDTQ600). Furthermore, the bond characteristics were characterized by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, ThermoFisherESCALAB250xi).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrodes were manufactured by mixing active materials with Super P and carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder into deionized water solvent under the mass ratio of 8:1:1 to form homogeneous black slurry. Subsequently, the slurry was spread on the surface of copper foil and dried at 60 °C for 12 h. Finally, the electrode was stamped into disks with a diameter of 10 mm and dried at 60 °C for another 6 h. All of the electrochemical capacities in this Article were mainly calculated based on the FeSe<sub>2</sub> mass in electrode. With sodium tablets (Aldrich) as the reference electrode and glass fiber membrane as the separator, 2032 type coin cells were assembled in a glovebox (MIKROUNA-Universal-2440-1750) filled with argon. One M NaClO<sub>4</sub> (Aldrich) in the mixed solvent of ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (EC:DMC = 1:1 in volume) with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as additive was chosen as the electrolyte system for SIBs. Cyclic voltammogram (CV) was conducted by an electrochemical measurement system (PARSTAT 2273) with a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> from 2.5 to 0.01 V. Meanwhile, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurement was carried out with the frequency range from 100 kHz to 10 mHz. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed by using a battery testing system (LAND CT2001A) within the potential of 0.01–2.5 V at room temperature.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">3.1 Structural Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p">The microstructures and morphologies of as-prepared raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites were investigated through FESEM and TEM. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b demonstrate that the FESEM images of the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products prepared in the absence of sulfur-doped GO suspensions display a microspheric shape assembled by numerous nanooctahedra FeSe<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles tightly with a diameter of 1–3 μm and have a relatively rough surface, which can be further identified by the TEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). As the FESEM images of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites show in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles with microspheric shape are embedded into the SG sheets. The SG sheets not only provide a substrate for the growth of FeSe<sub>2</sub>, but also hinder the particle aggregation of FeSe<sub>2</sub>. Meanwhile, the uniform distribution of FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles also could prevent the agglomeration of SG sheets to a certain extent, which can enhance the electrochemical performance for Na storage as a result of more exposed surfaces available for the reaction between active materials and sodium ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The microstructure of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites is further characterized by TEM as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f. It is clearly observed that microsphere-like FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles have a small size of 1–3 μm and the SG sheets with some wrinkles are rather thin and homogeneous.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12148d_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. FESEM images of (a, b) raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products and (c, d) FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites. TEM images of (e) raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products and (f) FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12148&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a represents the HRTEM image of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products, in which three lattice spacing values are measured to be approximately 0.304, 0.287, and 0.221 nm consistent with the (011), (101), and (210) crystal faces of FeSe<sub>2</sub>. From the HRTEM image of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b, it is found that a single lattice spacing about 0.257 nm is consistent with the (111) crystal plane of FeSe<sub>2</sub>, which is surrounded by the amorphous structure SG sheets, corresponding well with the FESEM and TEM images of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites. Meanwhile, the interlayer distance of SG sheets is measured to be 0.395 nm, which is larger than that of graphite crystal structure with 0.335 nm, which can be attributed to the introduction of sulfur atoms into the rGO sheets.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12148d_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products and (b) FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12148&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Examination of the crystalline phases of the samples was conducted by XRD. The XRD patterns of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G, and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a while the XRD patterns of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG samples prepared at 185 °C for 9, 12, and 15 h are presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S1</a>, which correspond well to the FeSe<sub>2</sub> standard card (JCPDS Card 65-2570), confirming the correct synthesis of FeSe<sub>2</sub> and the presence of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites. Furthermore, the main diffraction peaks of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG samples are similar to those of pure FeSe<sub>2</sub>, indicating the existence of SG sheets does not lead to the presences of new crystal orientations or changes in preferential orientations of FeSe<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46, 47)</a> which is mainly ascribed to the amorphous structure of rGO. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI Figure S2</a>, the Raman spectra of GO and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites exhibit two typical peaks at 1353 and 1603 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which are termed D band and G band, respectively. The value of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> increases from 0.836 (for GO) to 1.16 (for FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG), confirming the reduction of graphene oxide and the disordered structure of the rGO in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48, 49)</a> which is in accordance with the XRD results.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12148d_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns and (b) TGA curves of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> products, and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12148&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To calculate the precise loading of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites, the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG samples, and bare FeSe<sub>2</sub> were investigated. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, the weight loss of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> samples is 73.9%, 71.27%, and 64.36%, respectively, under the temperature range from 0 to 900 °C. The weight increases can be detected between 200 and 400 °C in the TG profiles of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG, and FeSe<sub>2</sub> due to the formation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and SeO<sub>2</sub> reacted by FeSe<sub>2</sub> with O<sub>2</sub>. Taking the curve of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG as an example, it suffers dramatic mass decline after 400 °C, which can be ascribed to the volatilization of SeO<sub>2</sub> and the combustion of SG. The weight reduction of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG is consistent with that of FeSe<sub>2</sub> and SG, which can be illustrated as the following equation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="ueq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m001.gif" alt=""></span><i>C</i> represents the weight of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites and <i>a</i> represents the loading content of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites. Thus, the precise content of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG samples can be calculated as 80.61%. Likewise, the accurate loading of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G samples is obtained as 73.23%.</div><div class="NLM_p">As displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, the elemental composition of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG products was characterized through EDX analysis. The table proves the element composition of the samples consists of C, O, S, Fe, and Se, while the atomic content ratio of Fe to Se is approximately 1:2, indicating the existence of sulfur atoms and the successful decoration of FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles on the surface of SG sheets. The elemental mapping images shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f, which was conducted by energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS), present the distribution of the element C, S, Fe, and Se in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG products, respectively. The evenly distributed points of C and S demonstrate a uniform distribution of sulfur atoms in the rGO structure. Additionally, the elemental mapping region of Fe overlaps with Se (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b), further verifying that the FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles are homogeneously distributed on the surface of SG sheets. With the purpose of investigating how sulfur atoms are covalently bonded to rGO sheets structure, XPS measurements were carried out. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g shows the XPS spectrum of S 2p, where two major peaks located at 161.49 and 167.04 eV can be observed, further confirming the presence of sulfur with the atomic percentage about 2.4 at %. Three fitted component peaks at 161.44, 165.34, and 167.29 eV are assigned to bonds of C–S–C, C–SO<sub><i>x</i></sub>–C, and C═S, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 43, 50 and 51">(43, 50, 51)</a> According to the results of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b and the XPS analysis of S 2p, it is concluded that doping sulfur atoms into the structure of rGO sheets can effectively enlarge the interlayer distances, which can guarantee Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation and facilitate the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> as a result of the large covalent radius of S atoms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12148d_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) FESEM-energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) images of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG products. (b–f) Element mapping images of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG products. (g) XPS spectra of S 2p in FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG products.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12148&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">To research the sodium storage behavior of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was tested with a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> from 0.01 to 2.5 V. The CV curves of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode are exhibited in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, in which there are three reduction peaks at around 1.13/0.66/0.32 V and three oxidation peaks at around 1.57/2.07/2.32 V observed in the first cycle of CV curves. In accordance with the previous reports about FeS<sub>2</sub> anodes for SIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 21, 23 and 24">(21, 23, 24)</a> the three discharge plateaus respectively imply the generation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeSe<sub>2</sub>, FeSe, and Na<sub>2</sub>Se, as well as Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>Se (reaction <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>). Meanwhile, the three charge plateaus can account for the formation of FeSe, the intermediates Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeSe<sub>2</sub>, and final reaction product FeSe<sub>2</sub>, respectively (reaction <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eqs 4">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="6">6</a>). The CV curves of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode remain stable overlaps from the second cycle, suggesting that the reaction keeps reversible and steady after the first cycle. The relevant chemical reaction equations are as follows:</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/medium/am-2015-12148d_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Cyclic voltammogram curves of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.5 V. (b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode within the first three cycles at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycle performance of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G, and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode under the voltage of 0.01–2.5 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/large/am-2015-12148d_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.5b12148&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Discharge process equation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m004.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span></div><div class="NLM_p">Charge process equation:<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m005.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m006.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq6"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/asset/images/am-2015-12148d_m007.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></span></div><div class="NLM_p">To further illustrate the reaction mechanism of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode, ex situ XRD measurements (shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) were performed on FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode at the different discharge and charge conditions. According to <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, the peak at 43° marked with α in the XRD patterns is assigned to the Cu current collector and the peaks marked with β are assigned to the polyimide film. For the pristine FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode without discharge/charge process, only the crystalline phase of FeSe<sub>2</sub> is detected (marked with γ), which means there are no chemical reactions and FeSe<sub>2</sub> can keep stable in air. When the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode is discharged to 0.8 V, the crystalline phases of FeSe<sub>2,</sub> FeSe (marked with δ), and Na<sub>2</sub>Se (marked with χ) are observed, indicating that the conversion reaction of FeSe<sub>2</sub> with Na<sup>+</sup> generates the crystals of FeSe and Na<sub>2</sub>Se. Upon reaching the potential of 0.2 V during the discharge process, the peaks of FeSe<sub>2</sub> are not discovered, while the crystal peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Se appearing at 22.5° are 37.3° are more pronounced and the crystal peaks of FeSe become slighter, demonstrating that the FeSe<sub>2</sub> in electrode is fully converted to FeSe and the FeSe continuously reacts with sodium ions to produce the crystalline phase of Na<sub>2</sub>Se. However, the peaks of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeSe<sub>2</sub> and Fe are not detected at the discharge states, which is mainly due to the low crystallinity of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeSe<sub>2</sub> and Fe. At the potential of 1.75 V in the charge process, the peak intensity of Na<sub>2</sub>Se decreases with the appearance of FeSe, suggesting the conversion reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>Se with Fe to produce the crystalline phase of FeSe. After being charged to 2.25 V, with the reappearance of FeSe<sub>2,</sub> the peak intensity of Na<sub>2</sub>Se is weaker accompanied by the disappearance of the rings of FeSe, proving that the reaction of FeSe with Na<sub>2</sub>Se generates the crystal of FeSe<sub>2</sub>. On the basis of the above analysis, the electrochemical reaction mechanism of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode can be determined, which is well consistent with the CV curves and confirming the high reversibility of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b displays the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode within the first three cycles from 0.01 to 2.5 V at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, which corresponds well to the CV curves. Additionally, the first discharge and charge capacity for FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode are 610.5 and 415 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, presenting a low initial Coulombic efficiency of 68%, which can be mainly attributed to the generation of solid electrolyte interface film, electrolyte decomposition, and the intercalation of the sodium ions into the interlayer of FeSe<sub>2</sub>. In subsequent cycles, the Coulombic efficiency of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode is promoted to more than 93% and remains at 98%. Furthermore, the CV and galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode under the same measure condition is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">The cycle performances of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G, and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode under the voltage of 0.01–2.5 V at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. Remarkably, the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode shows a higher reversible capacity and more steady cycle performance compared with the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub>. The discharge capacities of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> are 408 and 79.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, and the corresponding capacity retention rates counted since the second cycle are 90% and 17.5%, respectively. Being similar to FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode, the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode also exhibits a better cycling stability than raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> as a result of the existence of rGO matrix, which can relieve the volume change of the FeSe<sub>2</sub> in the conversion reaction process to avoid fast capacity fading. Meanwhile, based on the FeSe<sub>2</sub> mass in electrode, the specific capacity of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode is always lower than that of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode except the initial 4 cycles, which can be mainly attributed to the introduction of sulfur atoms into rGO structure. Additionally, the cycle performances of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G, and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode calculated by the whole mass of composites in electrode under the same test conditions have been measured to evaluate the contribution of G and SG to the composite electrodes. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode shows a higher reversible capacity of more than 400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> compared with FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode in the initial 35 cycles, which is mainly due to the lower sodium storage capacity of G or SG than that of FeSe<sub>2</sub>. After 35 cycles, the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode suffers a dramatic capacity decay and only 75.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can be obtained in the 100th cycle as a result of the volume change during the conversion reaction process, while the discharge capacities of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode are 370.9 and 263.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, respectively, exhibiting a better cycling stability than raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> due to the existence of rGO matrix. Nevertheless, based on the whole mass of composites in electrode, the specific capacity of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode is usually higher than that of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode, which can be mainly attributed to the higher loading of FeSe<sub>2</sub> in the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites and the introduction of sulfur atoms into rGO structure. Therefore, the enhanced cycling stability of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode can be mainly attributed to the rGO matrix, which can effectively improve the conductivity, buffer the volume change of the FeSe<sub>2</sub> during the conversion reaction process, generate more exposed surfaces available for Na storage, and restrain the aggregation of FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles, while the introduction of sulfur atoms into the rGO sheets can significantly improve the reversibly specific capacity of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode. To reveal the factors causing the faster capacity fading of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode, FESEM images of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode before and after cycling are investigated. As displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode still keeps relative structural integrity after 100 cycles, while the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode undergoes a distinct structural collapse, further demonstrating that rGO sheets can effectively alleviate the volume change of FeSe<sub>2</sub> caused by the conversion reactions. In addition, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> presents the cyclability of the bare G and SG electrode with the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 150 cycles. After 150 cycles, the SG electrode still possesses a recovered capacity of 135 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a high capacity retention of 80.7% can be obtained along with the high Coulombic efficiency, while the G electrode exhibits a reversible capacity of 101.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a high Coulombic efficiency of 98.5%. Consequently, the reversible capacity of SG electrode is higher than that of G electrode, which is mainly due to the covalently bonded S atoms to the rGO sheets. Meanwhile, based on the results of TGA, the precise contents of SG and G in the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G samples are 19.39% and 26.77%, respectively. Thus, the capacity contribution of SG to the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode is about 29.37 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, while the capacity contribution of G to the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode can be calculated to be 29.86 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> according to <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S5 and S7</a>. As another contrast, the SIBs performances of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode based on the same mass of FeSe<sub>2</sub> (about 64 wt %) in electrode under the same test conditions also have been provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, in which the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG materials possess a reversible discharge capacity of 408 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 discharge/charge cycles. In contrast, the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes, separately containing 80 wt % amount of FeSe<sub>2</sub> and 64 wt % amount of FeSe<sub>2</sub>, undergo an apparent capacity decline from the 30th cycle. After 100 cycles, the discharge capacities of the FeSe<sub>2</sub> (80 wt %) and FeSe<sub>2</sub> (64 wt %) are decreased to 75.8 and 134 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Notably, the specific capacity of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> (64 wt %) electrode is slightly higher than that of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode before 25 cycles and the cycling performance of FeSe<sub>2</sub> (64 wt %) electrode is more stable than that of FeSe<sub>2</sub> (80 wt %) electrode, which is mainly attributed to the existence of the redundant conductive carbon (SP). Furthermore, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode is much less than that of FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode, demonstrating that the incorporation of SG sheets can effectively enhance the conductivity of FeSe<sub>2.</sub></div><div class="NLM_p last">Moreover, as the rate performance is a significant standard to evaluate the quality of battery, the cycling performances of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub> under the different current densities of 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are estimated. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode delivers a reversible discharge capacity of 485.6, 476.2, 467.5, 446.4, 421.8, 383.3, and 277.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> matching with the current densities of 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Notably, when turned back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 70 cycles, the reversible capacity of 476.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can be maintained for another 30 cycles without obvious capacity loss, revealing an outstanding rate capability. In contrast, FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode with a poor rate performance presents a discharge capacity of 479.2, 366.3, 191.3, 31, 7.6, 5.6, and 15.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. After recovering back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity can reach 240.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the 72nd cycle, after which the specific capacity continuously keeps dropping to the 100th cycle. The excellent electrochemical performances of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode can be mainly ascribed to the following three factors: (1) the rGO sheets can improve the conductivity of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode and alleviate the volume expansion/contraction of microsphere-like FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles during the conversion reactions process; (2) the introduction of sulfur atoms into rGO structure can guarantee Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation and effectively improve the specific capacity of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode; (3) the synergistic effect between microsphere-like FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles and sulfur-doped rGO sheets.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we combine the high theoretical capacity materials FeSe<sub>2</sub> with sulfur-doped rGO sheets as anode materials of SIBs for the first time to achieve an excellent cycle performance and a remarkable rate capability simultaneously. To achieve these goals, we made nanooctahedra particles assembled FeSe<sub>2</sub> microspheres embedded into sulfur-doped rGO sheets through a one-step hydrothermal reduction route, in which the reduction of graphene oxide, sulfur doping, and preparation of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites are realized at the same time. The rGO matrix can effectively improve the conductivity of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites, alleviate the volume change of the microsphere-like FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles during the conversion reactions, generate more exposed surfaces available for Na storage, and restrain the aggregation of FeSe<sub>2</sub> particles. Meanwhile, the introduction of sulfur atoms into the rGO structure can enlarge the interlayer distances of rGO sheets, which can guarantee Na<sup>+</sup> accommodation and effectively improve the specific capacity of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode. The FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode employed as anode materials for SIBs exhibits an outstanding electrochemical property, which can present a reversible discharge capacity of 408 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, and a specific capacity of 277.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The excellent cycle performances and superior rate capabilities reveal that the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG hybrids hold great application prospect as a promising anode candidate for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.5b12148" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.5b12148</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Schematic of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG fabrication procedures; XRD patterns of raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG prepared at 185 °C for 9, 12, and 15 h, and the comparison of the XRD patterns of raw SG, raw FeSe<sub>2</sub>, and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG prepared at 185 °C for 12 h; Raman spectra of GO and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG composites; ex situ XRD patterns of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode tested at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in the first cycle at different states; cyclic voltammogram curves of the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> and the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 2.5 V and the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G electrode within the first three cycles at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; cycle performances of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG, FeSe<sub>2</sub>/G, and raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> based on the whole mass of composites in electrode; FESEM images of FeSe<sub>2</sub> and FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG electrode before and after 100 cycles; cycle performance of the G and SG electrodes at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; cycle performance of the FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG, raw FeSe<sub>2</sub> (64 wt %), and FeSe<sub>2</sub> (80 wt %) electrodes at the current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; electrochemical impedance spectra of FeSe<sub>2</sub>/SG and FeSe<sub>2</sub> electrode for SIBs after first cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.5b12148/suppl_file/am5b12148_si_001.pdf">am5b12148_si_001.pdf (2.21 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b12148" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges Facing Lithium Batteries and Electrical Double-Layer Capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Choi, Nam-Soon; Chen, Zonghai; Freunberger, Stefan A.; Ji, Xiulei; Sun, Yang-Kook; Amine, Khalil; Yushin, Gleb; Nazar, Linda F.; Cho, Jaephil; Bruce, Peter G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kajiyama, Masataka; Iwatate, Junichi; Nishikawa, Heisuke; Hitomi, Shuji; Okuyama, Ryoichi; Usui, Ryo; Yamada, Yasuhiro; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.  However, their reversible capacity is typically limited to 100 mA-h/g.  Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium Ion Insertion in Hollow Carbon Nanowires for Battery Applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Yuliang; Xiao, Lifen; Sushko, Maria L.; Wang, Wei; Schwenzer, Birgit; Xiao, Jie; Nie, Zimin; Saraf, Laxmikant V.; Yang, Zhengguo; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hollow carbon nanowires were prepd. by pyrolysis of a hollow polyaniline nanowire precursor.  The hollow carbon nanowires used as anode material for Na-ion batteries deliver a high reversible capacity of 251 mA-h/g and 82.2% capacity retention over 400 charge-discharge cycles between 1.2 and 0.01 V (vs. Na+/Na) at a const. current of 50 mA/g (0.2C).  Excellent cycling stability is also obsd. at an even higher charge-discharge rate.  A high reversible capacity of 149 mA-h/g also can be obtained at a current rate of 500 mA/g (2C).  The good Na-ion insertion property is attributed to the short diffusion distance in the hollow carbon nanowires and the large interlayer distance (0.37 nm) between the graphitic sheets, which agrees with the interlayered distance predicted by theor. calcns. to enable Na-ion insertion in carbon materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9utH1EdajJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixZ1qa2CpPDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XotlShu78%253D&amp;md5=c54fcb809a340c023d9185f1d9f5e848</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Palomares, V.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Serras, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Villaluenga, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hueso, K.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixZ1qa2CpPDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1039/c3cp52125g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Klein, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jache, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bhide, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Adelhelm, P.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Conversion Reactions for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys. Phys.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">15</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">15876</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">15887</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion reactions for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Klein, Franziska; Jache, Birte; Bhide, Amrtha; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">38</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15876-15887</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PPCPFQ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1463-9076</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on Na-ion batteries is focused on finding suitable electrode materials that enable cell reactions of high energy densities combined with low cost.  Naturally, an assessment of potential electrode materials requires a rational comparison with the analog reaction in Li-ion batteries.  The authors discuss the broad range of different conversion reactions for Na-ion batteries based on their basic thermodn. properties and compare them with their Li analogs.  Capacities, voltages, energy densities and vol. expansions are summarized to sketch out the scope for future studies in this research field.  For a given conversion electrode material, replacing Li by Na leads to a const. shift in cell potential ΔEo(Li-Na) depending on the material class.  For chlorides ΔEo(Li-Na) is nearly zero.  The theor. energy densities of conversion reactions of Na with fluorides or chlorides as pos. electrode materials typically reach values between 700 W h kg-1 and 1000 W h kg-1.  Next to the thermodn. assessment, results on several conversion reactions between Cu compds. (CuS, CuO, CuCl, CuCl2) and Na are being discussed.  Reactions with CuS and CuO were chosen because these compds. are frequently studied for conversion reactions with Li.  Chlorides are interesting because of ΔEo(Li-Na) ≈ 0 V As a result of chloride soly. in the electrolyte, the conversion process proceeds at defined potentials under rather small kinetic limitations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvFdD49Ctj47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixZ1qa2CpPDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsVWrtbrE&amp;md5=7135136abf30b3a83827d65de85c8f45</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C4CC09604E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, W.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A New High-Energy Cathode for a Na-Ion Battery with Ultrahigh Stability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, Young-Uk; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Kwon, Hyung-Soon; Kim, Byoungkook; Kim, Jongsoon; Kim, Haegyeom; Kim, Inkyung; Yoo, Han-Ill; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13870-13878</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Large-scale elec. energy storage is a key enabler for the use of renewable energy.  Recently, the room-temp. Na-ion battery has been re-highlighted as an alternative low-cost technol. for this application.  However, significant challenges such as energy d. and long-term stability must be addressed.  Herein, we introduce a novel cathode material, Na1.5VPO4.8F0.7, for Na-ion batteries.  This new material provides an energy d. of ∼600 Wh kg-1, the highest value among cathodes, originating from both the multielectron redox reaction (1.2 e- per formula unit) and the high potential (∼3.8 V vs Na+/Na) of the tailored vanadium redox couple (V3.8+/V5+).  Furthermore, an outstanding cycle life (∼95% capacity retention for 100 cycles and ∼84% for extended 500 cycles) could be achieved, which we attribute to the small vol. change (2.9%) upon cycling, the smallest vol. change among known Na intercalation cathodes.  The open crystal framework with two-dimensional Na diffusional pathways leads to low activation barriers for Na diffusion, enabling excellent rate capability.  We believe that this new material can bring the low-cost room-temp. Na-ion battery a step closer to a sustainable large-scale energy storage system.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpMgFNhDwY4a7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6lleiF8SyIw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXht1ymtLjL&amp;md5=dfcdb36e705456054d32760606a6f305</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1039/C5NR02064F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shang, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiao, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NaV<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> Nanosheet@Polypyrrole Core-Shell Composites with Good Electrochemical Performance as Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nanoscale</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">7</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">9261</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">9267</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NaV3O8 nanosheet@polypyrrole core-shell composites with good electrochemical performance as cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Hongyan; Liu, Yongchang; Shang, Minghui; Lu, Tianyu; Wang, Yijing; Jiao, Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Novel NaV3O8 nanosheet@polypyrrole core-shell composites have been successfully prepd. for the first time via a chem. oxidative polymn. method.  Based on the morphol. and microstructural characterization, it was found that polypyrrole (PPy) was uniformly wrapped on the surfaces of the NaV3O8 nanosheets.  When used as a cathode for Na-ion batteries, the as-synthesized NaV3O8@10% PPy composite showed significantly improved cycling performance (with a discharge capacity of 99 mA h g-1 after 60 cycles at 80 mA g-1) and better rate capacity (with a discharge capacity of 63 mA h g-1 at a high c.d. of 640 mA g-1) than pristine NaV3O8 nanosheets.  The greatly enhanced performance benefits from the unique core-shell structure, where the PPy coating not only prevents the pulverization and aggregation of the lamellar NaV3O8 nanosheets during cycling, which can improve the cycling stability, but also enhances the elec. cond. of the composite, which can facilitate Na+ ion diffusion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoIpRpX1Dm6s7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6lleiF8SyIw"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exfoliated-SnS2 restacked on graphene as a high-capacity, high-rate, and long-cycle life anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Kang, Hongyan; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Chengcheng; Cao, Kangzhe; Wang, Yijing; Yuan, Huatang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Designed as a high-capacity, high-rate, and long-cycle life anode for sodium ion batteries, exfoliated-SnS2 restacked on graphene is prepd. by the hydrolysis of lithiated SnS2 followed by a facile hydrothermal method.  Structural and morphol. characterizations demonstrate that ultrasmall SnS2 nanoplates (with a typical size of 20-50 nm) composed of 2-5 layers are homogeneously decorated on the surface of graphene, while the hybrid structure self-assembles into a three-dimensional (3D) network architecture.  The obtained SnS2/graphene nanocomposite delivers a remarkable capacity as high as 650 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  More impressively, the capacity can reach 326 mA h g-1 even at 4000 mA g-1 and remains stable at ∼610 mA h g-1 without fading up to 300 cycles when the rate is brought back to 200 mA g-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance is attributed to the synergetic effects between the ultrasmall SnS2 and the highly conductive graphene network.  The unique structure can simultaneously facilitate Na+ ion diffusion, provide more reaction sites, and suppress aggregation and vol. fluctuation of the active materials during prolonged cycling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRwuFHeQ2-ZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6lleiF8SyIw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhslelt7fN&amp;md5=8bd01685b22c4afe0f313378ef72187b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201301584"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yin, Y.-X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guo, Y.-G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wan, L.-J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Sandwich-Like Hierarchically Porous Carbon/Graphene Composite as a High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4033</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFEZ8wJYk1irVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh6lleiF8SyIw"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of sodium-ion battery was improved by using functionalized interconnected N-doped carbon nanofibers (FN-CNFs) as the anode.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical properties and discharge mechanism of Na/TiS2 cells with liquid electrolyte at room temperature</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ryu, Ho-Suk; Kim, Jong-Seon; Park, Jin-Soo; Park, Jin-Woo; Kim, Ki-Won; Ahn, Jou-Hyeon; Nam, Tae-Hyeon; Wang, Guoxiu; Ahn, Hyo-Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of Na/TiS2 cells fabricated using Na anodes and TiS2 cathodes and 1M sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate in tetraethylene glycol di-Me ether liq. electrolyte have been investigated.  The discharge curve has two plateau regions of 2.1 V and 1.6 V.  The loss of discharge capacity appeared mainly in the upper plateau region (2.0-2.6 V) and the discharge capacity in the low plateau region (0.8-2.0 V) does not decrease and is consistent during 40 cycles.  The discharge process between Na and TiS2 has a complex reaction mechanism, the intercalation at the upper plateau region and the conversion at the low plateau region.  Because, a distorted TiS2 phase appeared in the upper plateau, a new phase of NaxTiS2 was obsd. in the lower plateau, and the NaxTiS2 phase disappeared after full discharge as obsd. by x-ray diffraction and TEM.  The decrease of discharge capacity during cycling is a result of the formation of NaxTiS2 phase in the cathode after full charging and this phase accumulates by repeated cycling.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp4Oxeuj1-6xLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg6yuEl30E_wA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvFOiur8%253D&amp;md5=7f8304e44014d69c22b1c29f1ff87554</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1039/c3cc41934g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yue, J.
